  //<![CDATA[
    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }
    
    function initAppleId() {
      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',
        redirectURI: 'https://www.goodreads.com/apple_users/sign_in_with_apple_web',
        state: 'apple_oauth_state_a4be2457-24e8-4c5e-af8d-ae2f185729b9'
      });
    }
    
    var initializeSiwa = function() {
      var APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL =  "https://appleid.cdn-apple.com/appleauth/static/jsapi/appleid/1/en_US/appleid.auth.js"
      loadScript(APPLE_SIGN_IN_JS_URL, initAppleId);
    };
    if (typeof AppleID !== "undefined") {
      initAppleId();
    } else {
      initializeSiwa();
    }

<span id="freeTextContainer14497647493312650177"><b>The propulsive, shockingly plausible sequel to <i>New York Times</i> bestseller <i>Daemon</i>, the "Greatest. Techno-thriller. Period."*<br/>*William O'Brien, former director of cybersecurity and communications systems policy at the White House</b><br/><br/>2009 saw one of the most inventive techno-thriller debuts in decades as Daniel Suarez introduced his terrifying and tantalizing vision of a new world </span>
  <span id="freeText14497647493312650177" style="display:none"><b>The propulsive, shockingly plausible sequel to <i>New York Times</i> bestseller <i>Daemon</i>, the "Greatest. Techno-thriller. Period."*<br />*William O'Brien, former director of cybersecurity and communications systems policy at the White House</b><br /><br />2009 saw one of the most inventive techno-thriller debuts in decades as Daniel Suarez introduced his terrifying and tantalizing vision of a new world order. <i>Daemon</i> captured the attention of the tech community, became a national bestseller, garnered attention from futurists, literary critics, and the halls of government-leaving readers clamoring for the conclusion to Suarez's epic story.<br /><br />In the opening chapters of <i>Freedom™</i>, the Daemon is well on its way toward firm control of the modern world, using an expanded network of real-world, dispossessed darknet operatives to tear apart civilization and rebuild it anew. Civil war breaks out in the American Midwest, with the mainstream media stoking public fear in the face of this "Corn Rebellion." Former detective Pete Sebeck, now the Daemon's most famous and most reluctant operative, must lead a small band of enlightened humans in a populist movement designed to protect the new world order.<br /><br />But the private armies of global business are preparing to crush the Daemon once and for all. In a world of conflicted loyalties, rapidly diminishing government control, and a new choice between free will and the continuing comforts of ignorance, the stakes could not be higher: hanging in the balance is nothing less than democracy's last hope to survive the technology revolution.</span>
  <a data-text-id="14497647493312650177" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<div class='spoiler'>
This question contains spoilers…
<span class='spoilerAnswerText'>
<a href="/questions/354135-i-loved-this-series-but-one-question"><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[I loved this series, but one question still bothers me.<br />The Daemon "forced" reporter Anji Anderson into service and feeds her stories. So if she is a Daemon operative, why did her reporting seem supportive of the Major/Corporate agenda? If her propaganda stories about the Midwest were fed to her by the daemon, then what was the daemon's purpose in deceiving the general population? <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></a>
</span>
</div>

<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/51992762-cesar-felipe"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1468532010i/51992762._UX100_CR0,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/51992762-cesar-felipe">Cesar Felipe</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<div class='spoiler'>
This answer contains spoilers…
<span class='spoilerAnswerText'>
<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  
    This is a good question. At the beginning they discuss that Anji is talking about the decline of the dollar and the collapse of society, but at some p<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">…more</a>
    This is a good question. At the beginning they discuss that Anji is talking about the decline of the dollar and the collapse of society, but at some point the media as a whole stops talking about it and start saying that "everything is okay", including the attacks on darknet towns etc. Since Anji is hardly seen throughout the novel, an educated guess would be that the Major managed to take over the media as a whole as part of their plan, being able to control the civilans' perception of the darknets as terrorists, and this included circumventing the control of the Daemon over Anji Anderson, getting her on the Major's side. A scene or two actually depicting this would have been nice, though. <a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">(less)</a>
  
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>
</span>
</div>
</span>
</div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer86241137" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7292333751728210028">I have to say, I was a little disappointed in this book compared to "Daemon." It seemed a bit off the rails, very preachy, and without a central character to latch onto. Plus, the level of violence and gore was WAY over the top. I think the central messages are valid, super interesting, and high-level scary, but I'm not sure he gets them across as well as he could have. Kind of like burying an important social message inside Grand Theft Auto. You can do it, but most people are just laughing and </span>
  <span id="freeText7292333751728210028" style="display:none">I have to say, I was a little disappointed in this book compared to "Daemon." It seemed a bit off the rails, very preachy, and without a central character to latch onto. Plus, the level of violence and gore was WAY over the top. I think the central messages are valid, super interesting, and high-level scary, but I'm not sure he gets them across as well as he could have. Kind of like burying an important social message inside Grand Theft Auto. You can do it, but most people are just laughing and running down hookers. </span>

<div id="review_86574304" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/86574304" />
    <a title="Kemper" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/405390-kemper"><img alt="Kemper" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1495476851p2/405390.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/86574304?book_show_action=true">Jan 24, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kemper" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kemper" href="/user/show/405390-kemper">Kemper</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=cyber">cyber</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/405390-kemper?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer86574304" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3122660895641721663">After reading this book, I feel like I should put on a kilt, paint my face blue and stand in front of the corporate headquarters where I work. Then, waving my laptop over my head and doing my best Mel Gibson impersonation (without the anti-Semitism), I’ll shout, “You may take away our financial independence, our self-supporting communities and our personal privacy, but you’ll never take our FREEDOM!”<br/><br/>Too much?<br/><br/>Moving on…. This is a techno-thriller, but like the previous book <i> Daemon</i>, it’s a pretty</span>
  <span id="freeText3122660895641721663" style="display:none">After reading this book, I feel like I should put on a kilt, paint my face blue and stand in front of the corporate headquarters where I work. Then, waving my laptop over my head and doing my best Mel Gibson impersonation (without the anti-Semitism), I’ll shout, “You may take away our financial independence, our self-supporting communities and our personal privacy, but you’ll never take our FREEDOM!”<br /><br />Too much?<br /><br />Moving on…. This is a techno-thriller, but like the previous book <i> Daemon</i>, it’s a pretty damn ambitious techno-thriller. <i>Daemon</i> set the stage and now <i>Freedom ™</i> delivers the goods.<br /><br />Previously, a very wealthy computer guru known for the intricate games he devised named Matthew Sobol had died of cancer. But he left behind the Daemon, a computer program that works as a blueprint and recruiting tool for an economic and political revolution. Sobol was convinced that worldwide corporate corruption had reached a point where people were in danger of being nothing but puppets forever. <br /><br />By using the Daemon to seize the high ground on the internet, Sobol posthumously declared war on the existing forms of politics and finance. The Daemon also uses a virtual version of the Internet called the darknet to hook it’s operatives into a web overlay of the real world via HUD glasses and other biometric interfaces. <br /><br />After deliberately escalating the existing economic crisis, the Daemon uses the rules of on-line gaming and internet social media to begin creating a new society and economic model. And it uses high tech weaponry and willing operatives to defend the fledging darknet communities.<br /><br />A corporate shadow government doesn’t just want to destroy the Daemon and the darknet operatives, it wants to use the crisis the Daemon has created as an excuse to expand their power and influence to the point where traditional governments will be completely toothless. They use the mass media outlets to spin the story that the darknet communities are terrorists and using that as an excuse, unleash Blackwater-style private armies.<br /><br />The plot could come across as B-movie-ish, but Suarez manages to make even the more outlandish stuff sound reasonable, and he makes a lot of intriguing points about the current economic structure in areas like corporate farming. <br /><br />One of the cooler ideas is that on-line gaming rules and rating systems could be the basis for a new society. It’s sounds crazy, but Suarez makes an interesting argument that it could work. What if every person you dealt with had a visible ratings score based on their interactions with other people? Or if enough people instantly voted on a project being important, the group resources were allocated accordingly? <br /><br />And I like that Suarez doesn’t paint this as an instant utopia. The Daemon is definitely breaking a whole lot of eggs to make this omelet and some of it’s operatives are in danger of gaining far too much power, even within the rules of the system. <br /><br />A lively read with plenty of action and enough big ideas to make you think, I enjoyed this quite a bit. For anyone interested, I’d definitely read <i>Daemon</i> first.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3122660895641721663" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1766987140" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1766987140" />
    <a title="Bradley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley"><img alt="Bradley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508811508p2/4213258.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1766987140?book_show_action=true">Oct 19, 2016</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Bradley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Bradley" href="/user/show/4213258-bradley">Bradley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780451231895" href="/book/show/8488830-freedom">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=2016-shelf">2016-shelf</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4213258-bradley?shelf=worldbuilding-sf">worldbuilding-sf</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1766987140" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2485966541294782782">Nearly as amazing as the first book in the Duology, <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6665847.Daemon' title='Daemon by Daniel Suarez' rel='nofollow noopener'>Daemon</a>, it leads us right into the middle of an ideological breakdown or a breakthrough, with hoards of Daemon followers playing their lives as if it was all a huge game. And indeed, the way our economics and military and politics is run, it is just that. <br/><br/>So what happens when a game AI successfully outplays our gloriously flawed human nature all in the desire to prevent a total breakdown of our society, as all societies have broken down when ou</span>
  <span id="freeText2485966541294782782" style="display:none">Nearly as amazing as the first book in the Duology, <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/6665847.Daemon" title="Daemon by Daniel Suarez" rel="nofollow noopener">Daemon</a>, it leads us right into the middle of an ideological breakdown or a breakthrough, with hoards of Daemon followers playing their lives as if it was all a huge game. And indeed, the way our economics and military and politics is run, it is just that. <br /><br />So what happens when a game AI successfully outplays our gloriously flawed human nature all in the desire to prevent a total breakdown of our society, as all societies have broken down when our reach outstrips our grasp?<br /><br />Why, the old-guard, the rich, the staunch governmentalists, and the old idealists band together to take down, impossibly, the background program that had transformed the world. With devastating effect. Civil Wars, corn rebellions, tent cities, and absolute fear of the internet dominates this book.<br /><br />Oh yeah, and high level wizards (techno-kind) roam the world, having risen high in wealth and real power thanks to the Daemon, and they are truly awesome and rather scary. Sound like a game? Well, it is! But this system of rewards is all in real wealth, real influence, and really awesome tech.<br /><br />Who do I root for? *waves his wand around* <br /><br />I won't tell you.<br /><br />The fact is, this is still very much a techno-thriller to its core, but beyond that, it's super-ambitious and it's also a rather enormous SF undertaking in its own right, from the ideas, the social reform, or from the deeper implications of what it means to be human and so flawed as to have one stupid distributed program be able to outthink us, surprisingly so because it doesn't even have real intelligence!<br /><br />It's just programmed to manipulate us all really, really well. And I can't say I disagree with it's core purpose, either. <br /><br />But then, I must quote Robert A Heinlein, "Never underestimate the power of human stupidity."<br /><br />*sigh*<br /><br />Great book, great conclusion, and I don't even mind the soapbox that the author stood upon. SF is really all about ideas, but this one's a great story, too.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2485966541294782782" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2412746349" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2412746349" />

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2412746349?book_show_action=true">Oct 18, 2018</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780451231895" href="/book/show/8488830-freedom">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33449035-vignesh-ashok-kumar?shelf=mystery-thriller">mystery-thriller</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33449035-vignesh-ashok-kumar?shelf=scifi">scifi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33449035-vignesh-ashok-kumar?shelf=dystopian">dystopian</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33449035-vignesh-ashok-kumar?shelf=techno-thriller">techno-thriller</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2412746349" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8042895031795639331"><b> Rating - 7/10(Good) - Deliver us from Daemon....or not</b><br/><br/>This novel is a sequel to Daemon, so you have to probably read that first. Before reading this review, do check out my <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2412746269?book_show_action=false&from_review_page=1' rel='nofollow noopener'> review of Daemon </a>. <br/><br/>The first novel was definitely one of the best techno thrillers I have ever read and experienced. It had all the traits that a techno-thriller has. There were plethora of things that I learned. With that much excitement I had while reading the first novel, I started the second novel and I would say it was</span>
  <span id="freeText8042895031795639331" style="display:none"><b> Rating - 7/10(Good) - Deliver us from Daemon....or not</b><br /><br />This novel is a sequel to Daemon, so you have to probably read that first. Before reading this review, do check out my <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2412746269?book_show_action=false&amp;from_review_page=1" rel="nofollow noopener"> review of Daemon </a>. <br /><br />The first novel was definitely one of the best techno thrillers I have ever read and experienced. It had all the traits that a techno-thriller has. There were plethora of things that I learned. With that much excitement I had while reading the first novel, I started the second novel and I would say it was a total disappointment even though it was entertaining at times.<br /><br />While the first novel concentrated on the gory and so dark side of the Daemon, the second novel was completely contrary ie., it focused on Daemon being a good Samaritan. I was completely flabbergasted and taken aback by how the author completely flipped the script. It got me interested while I sensed that it was heading in that direction. But while I was reading, there were many things that didn't stick right. There were many time jumps which was a real pain and got me boring at times. The story didn't do justice for the main characters and it just invented many side characters who were totally monotonous. It felt like the scenes depicted were a cut and paste from various parts of the first novel at times. The pacing of the story was getting worse by every chapter: there were some chapters that made me dreary. The ending became more anti-climatic with no strong note and was flat. <br /><br />While the novel suffered from the above mentioned, it found more strength in the last part of the novel (except the ending) - the way the events branched were a thrilling roller coaster ride. There were more speculative technical ideas and the themes that were focused in this novel was mind blowing. The focus on the Virtual Reality and fantasy elements were more interesting and a delight.<br /><br />Overall, I would say this novel is an entertaining popcorn flick which could have made a real good addition to the duology if it wasn't for the downs mentioned above. :(</span>
  <a data-text-id="8042895031795639331" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Chris" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1881406-chris"><img alt="Chris" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1241892680p2/1881406.jpg" /></a>

    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer143222072" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer17027228678554093759"><a href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/65616320' rel='nofollow noopener'>Daemon</a> was so dark and depressing that I seriously considered skipping the sequel, but I'm glad I didn't. <a class='jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction'>(view spoiler)</a><span class='spoilerContainer' style='display: none'>[This book feels surprisingly different than its precursor: the first was all about how scary and damaging the Daemon was, but the second is all about how evil the corporate overlords are, and here the Daemon is clearly humanity's last hope to avoid <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/5470.1984' title='1984 by George Orwell' rel='nofollow noopener'>1984</a>. Everybody is coopted by last year's villain, as government is coopted by the oligarchs. <a class='jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction'>(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br/><br/>The biggest problem i</span>

Everybody is coopted by last year's villain, as government is coopted by the oligarchs. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />The biggest problem is that, for the book to be possible, the brilliant and insane Matthew Sobol must be a revolutionary genius in <strong>all</strong> the following areas: AI, game design, law/politics, economics, sociology, green technologies (with self-evidently brilliant priorities nobody has ever before raised), military planning, language (machine recognition, although he also designs at least one, like Tolkein!), application of technology (weapons, communications, fMRI, etc.), magic, precognition (he programs the Daemon to trigger on several events, but they are defined before his death). That part tips it over into fantasy -- if you were better at <strong>everything</strong> than anybody ever born, why then <em>yes</em>, you could change the world.<br /><br />Ironically, although <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4699575.Daemon__Daemon___1_" title="Daemon (Daemon, #1) by Daniel Suarez" rel="nofollow noopener">Daemon</a> was so dark &amp; perverse and Freedom isn't, Freedom is actually more depressing in a way, as Daemon shows us that in an absurd scenario, things can go to hell. On the other hand, <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[the whole premise of Freedom is that we are <em>already</em> too far down the road to hell and slavery to ever get back and, without a bloody armed revolution led by a dead god, we have no hope. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span></span>

<span id="freeTextContainer17800331042801712981">I listened to the audio version. When I finished Daemon, I was somewhat dissatisfied with the conclusion, as reflected in my review. I subsequently learned that Mr. Suarez could not find a publisher for Daemon, so published it himself, and it became an internet sensation. I feel that Freedom was better than Daemon, less repetitive and even more relevant to the financial crisis of 2008 and its aftermath, including the response of the Obama administration.   <span id="freeText17800331042801712981" style="display:none">I listened to the audio version. When I finished Daemon, I was somewhat dissatisfied with the conclusion, as reflected in my review. I subsequently learned that Mr. Suarez could not find a publisher for Daemon, so published it himself, and it became an internet sensation. I feel that Freedom was better than Daemon, less repetitive and even more relevant to the financial crisis of 2008 and its aftermath, including the response of the Obama administration. The story moves along quickly toward a satisfying resolution, although the question Sobel posed near the end isn't really one that needs to be answered "yes" or "no." The writing is excellent, although the author seems less comfortable handling romantic situations and conversations than when describing technology or creating action scenes.<br /><br />The narration is outstanding, and having a woman reading the headlines is a bonus. I felt the length was better matched to the subject matter than in Daemon, which seemed a bit too drawn out. I noticed that, in written form, Freedom was only 50 pages shorter than Daemon, a difference of a bit more than 10%, yet the audible version was more than 4 hours shorter, about 25% less. I didn't investigate why that is; probably the result of a different typesetting in print form.</span>

  <div class="left bodycol">

        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/100264044?book_show_action=true">Apr 27, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Danielle Adams" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Danielle Adams" href="/user/show/3645884-danielle-adams">Danielle Adams</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer100264044&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer100264044" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1302701070855417">I enjoyed Daemon a lot and was excited to jump right into Freedom (tm). However, I confess that I was a little disappointed. <br/><br/>What I really liked was the moral quandary aspect (I don't want to be too spoilery, so I'll stop there). Also, the virtual environment as everyday social fabric was nifty. Especially the contrast between the folks who treated this new world like they were actually IN a videogame, with all the magic and gods imagery that often brings, and the "regular" people who didn't fus</span>
  <span id="freeText1302701070855417" style="display:none">I enjoyed Daemon a lot and was excited to jump right into Freedom (tm). However, I confess that I was a little disappointed. <br /><br />What I really liked was the moral quandary aspect (I don't want to be too spoilery, so I'll stop there). Also, the virtual environment as everyday social fabric was nifty. Especially the contrast between the folks who treated this new world like they were actually IN a videogame, with all the magic and gods imagery that often brings, and the "regular" people who didn't fuss with the pomp and circumstance of it all. <br /><br />I'll admit that some of the tech elements were on the extreme side, but I don't mind nearish-future tech making its way into my contemporary fiction as long as it's cool. <br /><br />What I didn't like...gosh, there was a lot. It was WAY to preachy. Much too much time was spent telling us what was wrong with society in long monologues rather than letting character actions and events get us there. I really wish I'd been able to see more of the Unnamed One's quest and I really wanted to know more about Loki and the cult of Roy Merritt. Also, the action sequences kept teasing me with hints of what they could have been.<br /><br />My fix? Let the characters and the action take center stage and let the message sneak up on the reader in the background.</span>
  <a data-text-id="1302701070855417" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

    <a title="Michael" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1939991-michael"><img alt="Michael" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

              <span id="reviewTextContainer85972369" class="readable"

<span id="freeTextContainer9630380949518302847">Tough rating, probably deserves 2.5 stars.<br/><br/>This is the sequel to Daemon, which I recall enjoying a good bit. Daemon was pretty popular among computer programmers for its (according to the description here at goodreads) "shockingly plausible" premise. It's a techno-thriller without the techno-babble.<br/><br/>Freedom is, I think, meant to be in the same mold, although I'm not sure what an encrypted IP beacon is. That is to say, the techno gets a little more babbly.<br/><br/>I did love the premise. In Daemon, an out o</span>
  <span id="freeText9630380949518302847" style="display:none">Tough rating, probably deserves 2.5 stars.<br /><br />This is the sequel to Daemon, which I recall enjoying a good bit. Daemon was pretty popular among computer programmers for its (according to the description here at goodreads) "shockingly plausible" premise. It's a techno-thriller without the techno-babble.<br /><br />Freedom is, I think, meant to be in the same mold, although I'm not sure what an encrypted IP beacon is. That is to say, the techno gets a little more babbly.<br /><br />I did love the premise. In Daemon, an out of control computer program tries to take over the world. It's the enemy. In Freedom, things are a little more complicated.<br /><br />Still, lots of the issues feel half-baked. It's great to imagine a utopia of hyper-local communities, but where are the ones outside of Iowa going to get their food? How's it going to work with cities?<br /><br />Also, there's only one female character and she's pretty much a passive observer.<br /><br />So it gets rounded down to two stars. Disappointing given my memory of Daemon. </span>
  <a data-text-id="9630380949518302847" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer218128649522623760">I was skeptical that Suarez could recapture the utter immersion and fascinating construction of Daemon, but he did. I think one of the main ways he did so, was turning from an emphasis on technology to an emphasis on the socio-political themes that began to emerge in Daemon.<br/><br/>In the tradition of Brave New World and 1984, Freedom(TM) serves as a warning by exaggeration. It's not that the events and revelations of Freedom(TM) are accurate or even prescient, it's that the elements that could cause th</span>
  <span id="freeText218128649522623760" style="display:none">I was skeptical that Suarez could recapture the utter immersion and fascinating construction of Daemon, but he did. I think one of the main ways he did so, was turning from an emphasis on technology to an emphasis on the socio-political themes that began to emerge in Daemon.<br /><br />In the tradition of Brave New World and 1984, Freedom(TM) serves as a warning by exaggeration. It's not that the events and revelations of Freedom(TM) are accurate or even prescient, it's that the elements that could cause them to happen all exist right now. <br /><br />The confluence of events that could make the world just like the one in Freedom(TM) are certainly unlikely, but that doesn't mean we shouldn't pay attention to the warning.</span>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,

              <span id="reviewTextContainer86658847" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7366752672120842670">Where to begin?<br/><br/>Well, let's go with characters. The characters are cardboard cut-outs, who speak almost entirely in either cliches, or chunks of exposition that frequently confers data that every character involved in the conversation already knows and furthermore, the reader knows as well.<br/><br/>The book's plot takes several skips forward in time. Part 2: Eight Months Later! etc. Those blank spaces of time is where any character growth--changes in allegiances or beliefs or feelings at one another, all </span>
  <span id="freeText7366752672120842670" style="display:none">Where to begin?<br /><br />Well, let's go with characters. The characters are cardboard cut-outs, who speak almost entirely in either cliches, or chunks of exposition that frequently confers data that every character involved in the conversation already knows and furthermore, the reader knows as well.<br /><br />The book's plot takes several skips forward in time. Part 2: Eight Months Later! etc. Those blank spaces of time is where any character growth--changes in allegiances or beliefs or feelings at one another, all that human development stuff--takes place.<br /><br />The central titular theme is supposed to be the proving that the enlightened society ("the darknet") that "the daemon" (the magical botnet that Sobol programmed to handle every contingency possible ever all in a couple years before getting done in by brain cancer) is creating. Will the people have freedom(tm)? Or will the daemon rule them with an iron digital fist?<br /><br />Only there's just a single darknet fellow who's against Freedom(tm). That'd be Loki Stormbringer, an ex-rave organizer/hacker who became one of the daemon's highest-level operatives. He commands a fleet of robot cars and motorcycles with scythe blades, kinetic-kill dart delivery weather balloons, has personal laser weaponry, etc. He's supposed to be a dangerous psychopath, but in this case it means he's a cardboard cutout with "dangerous psychopath" printed on it in big block letters in crayon. Every single other darknet character the reader will encounter at any time hates Loki and all he stands for. So the quest to justify freedom is pretty much predetermined, which is one of the many unintentionally funny things going on.<br /><br />Things get progressively sillier. The token badguy darknet gets maimed, but his pet AI Nazi program he set free (I don't know, man, I just read these things), Herr Oberstleutenant I'llBeVillainInTheThirdBook or something like that somehow results in him getting bionic implants. The real bad guys try to use a code exploit in the daemon to use it for their own purposes, but of course it was programmed for exactly the way they used it, the security flaw was a trojan horse and it dramatically turns the tables on them. Loki's reign of terror is brought to an end by a good AI program based on an FBI guy the darknet turned into a folk hero, some other things happen, the aforementioned AI Nazi program gives "The Major" (the frontman for the International Bankers, more or less) his comeuppance in an epilogue, and the only thing missing is a big THE END??? on the final page.<br /><br />It's not a good book. I may have been a little subtle about that. But it's that species of bad that manages the trick of wrapping right around and becoming a sort of twisted awesome, in a way that had me laughing out loud at multiple points from how inept moment after moment was.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7366752672120842670" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29075798-adam?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29075798-adam?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29075798-adam?shelf=narration-4-star">narration-4-star</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29075798-adam?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29075798-adam?shelf=read-2018">read-2018</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/29075798-adam?shelf=story-3-star">story-3-star</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2576248756" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7834941416411257614">Review of the audiobook narrated by Jeff Gurner.<br/><br/>I read Daemon in 2014 and loved it so much that I immediately followed up by starting Freedom, but was never grabbed by it and as a result it had been sitting at DNF 39% for four years. I was hoping that restarting the book, this time in audiobook format, would bring me back to what I loved so much about the first book. That was not the case, as I ended up with much the same impression as I was left with when abandoning the book four years ago. <br/><br/>The</span>
  <span id="freeText7834941416411257614" style="display:none">Review of the audiobook narrated by Jeff Gurner.<br /><br />I read Daemon in 2014 and loved it so much that I immediately followed up by starting Freedom, but was never grabbed by it and as a result it had been sitting at DNF 39% for four years. I was hoping that restarting the book, this time in audiobook format, would bring me back to what I loved so much about the first book. That was not the case, as I ended up with much the same impression as I was left with when abandoning the book four years ago. <br /><br />There are aspects of this book that I love, such as the technology and Suarez's ideas about its effect on society. The problem is that the characters and plot that drive these interesting concepts are not themselves interesting. There are no primary characters to connect or identify with. Instead, all of the POV characters feel like secondary characters. The action sequences are far too frenetic to be exciting in any way. They're more like a big budget action movie with lots of explosions and not much depth.<br /><br />Jeff Gurner is excellent at giving each of the different characters distinct voices. In the end though I thought it was just a good performance, although that could be in part due to my lack of enjoyment for the story.<br /><br />Final verdict: 3 star story, 4 star narration, 3 stars overall</span>
  <a data-text-id="7834941416411257614" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Shalor T." class="user" itemprop="url" name="Shalor T." href="/user/show/4436152-shalor-t">Shalor T.</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer132387691" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3465154557794463388">I'll give you a bit of a warning right now. I'm going to discuss a bit of the plot of `Daemon'. If you haven't read that book, then you may want to hesitate before reading this review. If you simply want to know whether I enjoyed this book & if you should purchase this, then the answer is yes. I loved this book & you absolutely have to buy it. However I will warn you- the very basic outline of `Freedom (tm)' will give spoilers on the ending of `Daemon'. You've been warned, so on with the review.</span>
  <span id="freeText3465154557794463388" style="display:none">I'll give you a bit of a warning right now. I'm going to discuss a bit of the plot of `Daemon'. If you haven't read that book, then you may want to hesitate before reading this review. If you simply want to know whether I enjoyed this book &amp; if you should purchase this, then the answer is yes. I loved this book &amp; you absolutely have to buy it. However I will warn you- the very basic outline of `Freedom (tm)' will give spoilers on the ending of `Daemon'. You've been warned, so on with the review.<br /><br /><br />In `Daemon', we were introduced to genius computer mogul Matthew Sobol or rather, to his computer generated avatar. We learned that he had unleashed a computer creation that threatened to overthrow society. A few select individuals knew about this, some of them working for the daemon, some against it. Some of those individuals were wrongly accused of creating the daemon, with one of them- Pete Sebeck, being executed for it. Now we get to learn exactly what Sobol's long term plans were as Sebeck (resuscitated at the end of `Daemon') is commanded to do a seemingly impossible task: to justify the freedom of humanity or risk being under the control of the daemon forever.<br /><br />Meanwhile the hyper intelligent hacker Gragg (now known as Loki Stormbringer) is on a hunt for the Major. Even as he continues his hunt, he alienates himself from the rest of the darknet community &amp; runs the risk of doing the daemon project more harm than good. The roguish Jon Ross is still on the run &amp; his would be paramour Natalie Phillips is torn between joining him or staying with her government... even as it begins to fall around her. <br /><br />I absolutely loved this book on two different levels. On one level you have the sheer enjoyment of a techno/sci-fi thriller. You can't beat the energy in this book- it's incredibly fun to read. On the other level, you have a story with a hidden allegory. It makes you question whether change would show up as good or bad. At one point of the book, a character equates the daemon's change with the settlers inhabiting the United States, saying that at the time, the change was not good for everyone but turned into something big. <br /><br />The narrative continues to jump from person to person, which at times made me a little frustrated because I wanted to stay with one specific person to see where they were going. I'll admit it- I had favorites amongst the characters, (mostly Sebeck &amp; the sociopathic Loki) which is pretty much the only reason I'd gripe about the shift in perspectives. People who complained about the unrealistic technology in the first book should be warned that it doesn't change in this volume. (Hence the reason I use the term "sci-fi" when describing what type of book this is.) You see more of it in this book, with new add-ons that I won't describe here, mostly for spoiler reasons. <br /><br />I read through this book pretty quickly during the Thanksgiving break, mostly because after the cliffhanger in the previous book, I just REALLY wanted to know what happened next. I will admit that while I enjoyed this book immensely, the ending was a little weak. There is the potential for another book if Suarez wants to write one, although if not, there's enough of an ending to satisfy me. If he does write one, I can say that I will read it. I really liked the imagery as well as the implications of several key characters at the end of the book. (I can't go into detail, but it's safe to say that they ended up as larger than life characters.)<br /><br />Fans of the previous book will enjoy this book. If you didn't like it, then you may want to skip it- it's more of the same. And if you haven't read the previous book then I recommend skipping this book until you've finished `Daemon'- otherwise you will be terribly lost plot-wise.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3465154557794463388" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_169159984" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/169159984" />
    <a title="Leon M" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3186291-leon-m"><img alt="Leon M" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/169159984?book_show_action=true">May 18, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Leon M" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Leon M" href="/user/show/3186291-leon-m">Leon M</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3186291-leon-m?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer169159984" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9916792128377653359">"Freedom (TM)" is a beautiful and awe-inspiring sequel to Daniel Suarez highly successful "Daemon". Having laid all the groundwork in "Daemon", Suarez uses most of this book to use this foundation in order to explore a new concept of social organization based on empowering information technology. <br/><br/>The new society emerging in "Freedom (TM)" is based on the darknet, an alternative to the internet using fast wireless meshes in order to increase the durability and availability of the network. People </span>
  <span id="freeText9916792128377653359" style="display:none">"Freedom (TM)" is a beautiful and awe-inspiring sequel to Daniel Suarez highly successful "Daemon". Having laid all the groundwork in "Daemon", Suarez uses most of this book to use this foundation in order to explore a new concept of social organization based on empowering information technology. <br /><br />The new society emerging in "Freedom (TM)" is based on the darknet, an alternative to the internet using fast wireless meshes in order to increase the durability and availability of the network. People who join the darknet are assigned a role in society according to their interests and skills. While they fulfill these roles, they can advance their "levels" of skill (as measured by the level-number on their augmented reality callout). Users give reputation rankings to each other for most interactions, giving everybody an incentive to be cooperative and helpful. Decisions in society are made by those who have high levels (only if they are on a concrete missions assigned by society), all of society together in votes reminiscent of direct democracy, or - concerning more important and top priority tasks - even by the computer system itself. This system of IT-based democracy was one of the most advanced ideas of societal organization that I read about in a novel. It reminded me of similar ideas coming from the Zeitgeist Movement and other organizations proposing IT government as in "it is the computer that makes the decisions".<br /><br />Another thing that struck me about the book was the clarity with which the corporate elite was depicted. I must be careful here, because it might just be me reading the same books as the author (judging from his "Recommended Reading" list at the end of the book), but I found the description of corporate influence on governments worldwide highly convincing. The chapters that take the form of discussions between NSA, FBI, BSM and private consulting agencies are especially illuminating and interesting.<br /><br />Coming to a close, I have to say that this is quite probably as good a contemporary novel as you can get. Don't make the mistake to categorize this as "just another of this futuristic scifi novels", as Suarez really hides a discussion of contemporary society behind the sci-fi backdrop. If you are interested in the future, in information technology and/or societal organization, read this novel - you might even try to skip the prequel to "Freedom (TM)" as this is by far the better of the two!</span>
  <a data-text-id="9916792128377653359" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_414504755" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/414504755" />
    <a title="Mogsy (MMOGC)" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/4379514-mogsy-mmogc"><img alt="Mogsy (MMOGC)" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1286325553p2/4379514.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/414504755?book_show_action=true">Sep 14, 2012</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mogsy (MMOGC)" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mogsy (MMOGC)" href="/user/show/4379514-mogsy-mmogc">Mogsy (MMOGC)</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4379514-mogsy-mmogc?shelf=dystopia">dystopia</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4379514-mogsy-mmogc?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4379514-mogsy-mmogc?shelf=mystery">mystery</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4379514-mogsy-mmogc?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/4379514-mogsy-mmogc?shelf=thriller-suspense">thriller-suspense</a>,

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer414504755" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11293316461971607981">I'd thought the first book was dark, but wow, Freedom (TM) takes it even further. Anyway, high marks on the story, but downgraded to 3 stars because I can't say it was the satisfactory conclusion I expected. I mentioned in the review of Daemon that in the second half of the book everything seemed to wind down, and I had hoped Freedom (TM) would rekindle it again. <br/><br/>It didn't play out that way, unfortunately. Mainly, it was because I felt many of the characters we met in the first book were relegat</span>
  <span id="freeText11293316461971607981" style="display:none">I'd thought the first book was dark, but wow, Freedom (TM) takes it even further. Anyway, high marks on the story, but downgraded to 3 stars because I can't say it was the satisfactory conclusion I expected. I mentioned in the review of Daemon that in the second half of the book everything seemed to wind down, and I had hoped Freedom (TM) would rekindle it again. <br /><br />It didn't play out that way, unfortunately. Mainly, it was because I felt many of the characters we met in the first book were relegated to the background in Freedom (TM). for example, characters like Ross disappear for long stretches at a time while new ones I didn't really care for were introduced. Natalie Philips, pretty much the only female character in these books, also felt completely useless and wasted. Even the presence of Matthew Sobol appears to have diminished, and it was the all powerfulness of his Daemon in the first book that made it such a thrilling read in the first place.<br /><br />What this sacrifice bought, however, was a more in depth look at the Darknet and in the lives of people living in these semi-cyber reality societies that we only got a glimpse of in the first book. The concept is kinda cool, actually -- sort of like living in an online game come to life. <br /><br />Speaking of which, the science and technology has also been dialed up big time. Despite the sci-fi nature of these two books, I find it very interesting how half of the reviews I've read talk about the plausibility of such a scenario, while the other half find it too farfetched and unrealistic. Admittedly, I fall into the latter group, but then again I'm no software designer or network systems expert. I suppose it all comes down to the reader, and his or her interests and knowledge in the novel's topics. <br /><br />If there's one big gripe I have about this book, it's that at times it could get very "preachy". I find this often happens with books involving groups of people trying to reconstruct civilization and build their own utopian societies. The author invests so much into describing the mission and trying to convince the reader, when really, I'm more interested in these ideas being shown rather than pounded in my face. In my opinion the time could also have been better spent, say, maybe developing the characters involving them more in the plot?<br /><br />In any case, these two books constituted a very unique techno thriller, well worth the read.</span>
  <a data-text-id="11293316461971607981" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<div id="review_102130024" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/102130024" />
    <a title="Ian" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/933334-ian"><img alt="Ian" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1203721486p2/933334.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/102130024?book_show_action=true">May 10, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ian" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ian" href="/user/show/933334-ian">Ian</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/933334-ian?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/933334-ian?shelf=suspense">suspense</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/933334-ian?shelf=action">action</a>
          </div>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer102130024" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6687379766590981087">Freedom (TM) is an excellent sequel and a fantastic book in its own right, but I don't want that to detract you from reading Daemon first (so make sure you do that). Needless to say when I finished Daemon I'd was very eager to read its sequel. As soon as I saw that Freedom (TM) was released I snatched at the opportunity to listen to the audiobook version, which is just as excellent as the first book, and I found that I was in for more than I expected.<br/><br/>Any good book can be entertaining, a truly ex</span>
  <span id="freeText6687379766590981087" style="display:none">Freedom (TM) is an excellent sequel and a fantastic book in its own right, but I don't want that to detract you from reading Daemon first (so make sure you do that). Needless to say when I finished Daemon I'd was very eager to read its sequel. As soon as I saw that Freedom (TM) was released I snatched at the opportunity to listen to the audiobook version, which is just as excellent as the first book, and I found that I was in for more than I expected.<br /><br />Any good book can be entertaining, a truly excellent book will draw you in and take over your thoughts for the rest of the week. I think that I can safely say that Freedom (TM) has done that for me and I'll be lucky if I only continue thinking about it for the rest of this week. <br /><br />Freedom (TM) delivered on all the things that I loved about Daemon - there's cool technology, videogame-based action and characters, the story is brilliant, engaging and extremely suspenseful, and the plot as a whole isn't predictable; but it certainly is plausible. What I couldn't have expected was that the main concept that is presented in this story is so intriguing that I actually want to try it out.<br /><br />Building on the structure that was introduced in Daemon, Daniel Suarez developed the dark net into a new self-sufficient societal model that incorporates advanced technology and social media. The best way to compare it would be to say it is a cross between Facebook, Digg, YouTube, a farmers market, and Star Wars (crazy I know but trust me it works). Against this Suarez places the capitalist, corporate environment that controls food, security, and influences government as the very real-life villain. While the depictions of this villain are certainly extreme, again what makes this book so fascinating and worthwhile is the plausibility of it all. Reading this book you have to wonder just how controlled by corporate interests we all are... And whether or not freedom really is trademarked.<br /><br />I hope everyone gets a chance to read and enjoy this book; I plan on rereading it a few more times.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6687379766590981087" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_201660853" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/201660853" />
    <a title="Terry" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1717479-terry"><img alt="Terry" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1558225879p2/1717479.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/201660853?book_show_action=true">Aug 24, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Terry" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Terry" href="/user/show/1717479-terry">Terry</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1717479-terry?shelf=technology">technology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1717479-terry?shelf=science-fiction">science-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/1717479-terry?shelf=thriller">thriller</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer201660853" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer7141317494589857624">Freedom(TM) is the sequel to Daemon that patches a number of holes the writer left in the first book. The prose is tighter, the descriptions more direct, the characters are fleshed out and the plot is nicely wound down. If you enjoyed Daemon, there is no reason not to read the sequel.<br/><br/>Writing:<br/>*The action, even combat, was easier for the reader to track. Details were left to essentials and depictions of gore and violence were in a range I enjoy.<br/><br/>*The tech talk was less computer-centric and focused </span>
  <span id="freeText7141317494589857624" style="display:none">Freedom(TM) is the sequel to Daemon that patches a number of holes the writer left in the first book. The prose is tighter, the descriptions more direct, the characters are fleshed out and the plot is nicely wound down. If you enjoyed Daemon, there is no reason not to read the sequel.<br /><br />Writing:<br />*The action, even combat, was easier for the reader to track. Details were left to essentials and depictions of gore and violence were in a range I enjoy.<br /><br />*The tech talk was less computer-centric and focused on broader families of technologies. When these new items were brought up, knowing the details of how say a Indium-Gallium-Arsenide composite was formed is a good flourish but not knowing doesn't harm you.<br /><br />Plotting:<br />*The integration of MMO elements to everyday living is near masterful. A meatspace equivalent of magical items, NPCs, Area of Effect weapons, questing, and leveling are all represented in believable and non-cheesy methods.<br /><br />*The range of participation from the hardcore darknet participant to the friends of the darknet to dogs are done well. I expected the range of interactions to be very digital and consisting of people being "in or out" and was glad this wasn't the case.<br /><br />*The cloudgate quest concept was well done with the thread of the quest being not places but events.<br /><br />*The "hey dummies, this is what's happening" chapters were less overbearing.<br /><br />Problems:<br />*The ending was too tidy.<br /><br />*The price of victory seemed quite low.<br /><br />*I think some liberties were taken with what would actually happen if gas hit $17 a gallon, unemployment hit 30%, and how international markets would be affected.<br /><br />*Still think the katana-wielding motorcycles were excessive.</span>
  <a data-text-id="7141317494589857624" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

    <a title="Mattazuma" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/103984-mattazuma"><img alt="Mattazuma" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/88967498?book_show_action=true">Feb 09, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Mattazuma" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Mattazuma" href="/user/show/103984-mattazuma">Mattazuma</a>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer88967498" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer3565495052011334425">I read this book in 2 days. I blew off all of the Super Bowl pregame stuff to finish the book. It is the sequel to/continuation of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4699575.Daemon' title='Daemon by Daniel Suarez' rel='nofollow noopener'>Daemon</a>. It documents a possible kinda distopian future.<br/><br/>I liked Freedom more than Daemon. I think it's mostly because part of the first book is to set up the world and the characters in it. This book just rolls from the beginning. You really have to read Daemon first to know what's going on.<br/><br/>For a sci-fi-ish techno-thriller, there is a lot of <a href='http://bit.ly/9tdvVK' rel='nofollow noopener'>King Corn</a> and <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3109.The_Omnivore_s_Dilemma_A_Natural_History_of_Four_Meals' title='The Omnivore's Dilemma A Natural History of Four Meals by Michael Pollan' rel='nofollow noopener'>The Omnivore</a></span>
  <span id="freeText3565495052011334425" style="display:none">I read this book in 2 days. I blew off all of the Super Bowl pregame stuff to finish the book. It is the sequel to/continuation of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4699575.Daemon" title="Daemon by Daniel Suarez" rel="nofollow noopener">Daemon</a>. It documents a possible kinda distopian future.<br /><br />I liked Freedom more than Daemon. I think it's mostly because part of the first book is to set up the world and the characters in it. This book just rolls from the beginning. You really have to read Daemon first to know what's going on.<br /><br />For a sci-fi-ish techno-thriller, there is a lot of <a href="http://bit.ly/9tdvVK" rel="nofollow noopener">King Corn</a> and <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/3109.The_Omnivore_s_Dilemma_A_Natural_History_of_Four_Meals" title="The Omnivore's Dilemma A Natural History of Four Meals by Michael Pollan" rel="nofollow noopener">The Omnivore's Dilemma</a> in it. Trust me, it makes a lot of sense, and honestly made me think about some 'localvore' ideas I hadn't really considered before. This book also kind of reminded me of the <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/29579.Foundation__Foundation___1_" title="Foundation (Foundation, #1) by Isaac Asimov" rel="nofollow noopener">Foundation</a> trilogy, which is another personal favorite.<br /><br />The way the book wraps up, I'm not sure there's another book coming. I really really hope Daniel Suarez is working on another volume in the series.<br /><br />This is an excellent book. Yeah, it's only in hardcover right now, but I am really glad I bought it and I think you shouldn't miss out on a very smart book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="3565495052011334425" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer3881436196510990432">"Freedom" follows the incredibly good "Daemon" which made people turn heads. Freedom is itself one of those books that is hard to put down forcing you to read-on way past self-imposed bedtimes.<br/>What both novels have is an ethereal evil or good? Much depends on your point of view here; but its the driving force behind most actions in the books.   <span id="freeText3881436196510990432" style="display:none">"Freedom" follows the incredibly good "Daemon" which made people turn heads. Freedom is itself one of those books that is hard to put down forcing you to read-on way past self-imposed bedtimes.<br />What both novels have is an ethereal evil or good? Much depends on your point of view here; but its the driving force behind most actions in the books. We follow a number of characters that we all loved from "Daemon" and the one character that was lost has now become bigger in death than he ever was in life. He embodies that persona we seem to need a true Hero.<br />Sometimes I do feel the Darknet is way to wholesome and good. Loki does help. But a grittier Darknet would have suited Freedom better like it did in Daemon. <br /><br />Who should read this book? Everyone Daemon first follow by Freedom. Fans of Crichton (yes it is an apt comparison), fans of Vernor Vinge, fans of Dan Simmons will find Daniel Suarez to be really entertaining. Any science fiction fan that loves the novels that envision a better future, those that aspire to greatness, one were humanity reclaims its sense from the ever encroaching corporate monopolies will simply love it.<br /><br />I'll go back to one of my favorite:<br /><br />“Whether you see the world as emergent or, deteriorating. We have long known that some people favor innovation and look positively toward the future while others are frightened of change and want to halt innovation.” - Michael Crichton</span>
