<div id="review_620553090" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/620553090" />
    <a title="Erica" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/2644090-erica"><img alt="Erica" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1558548351p2/2644090.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/620553090?book_show_action=true">May 20, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Erica" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Erica" href="/user/show/2644090-erica">Erica</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Audiobook 9781615871100" href="/book/show/7780959-freedom">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2644090-erica?shelf=audiobook">audiobook</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2644090-erica?shelf=cults">cults</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2644090-erica?shelf=future-fiction">future-fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2644090-erica?shelf=inside-vs-outside">inside-vs-outside</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2644090-erica?shelf=men-and-boys">men-and-boys</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2644090-erica?shelf=video-games">video-games</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2644090-erica?shelf=leadership">leadership</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2644090-erica?shelf=mythology">mythology</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2644090-erica?shelf=war-stories">war-stories</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/2644090-erica?shelf=unengaging">unengaging</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer620553090" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer9583478248823398972">Ha! I'm listening to the part where Ross is visiting Hank's farm (don't worry, that's not a spoiler)(and I'm cracking up that the reader gave Farmer Hank the voice of Hank Hill ("King of the Hill"))<br/>EN EE way...so Hank is explaining how they're using crops and animals to restore the soil and how chickens run along behind to peck worms and grubs from the dirt and I am almost convinced that Suarez got this whole passage from one of <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2623.Joel_Salatin' title='Joel Salatin' rel='nofollow noopener'>Joel Salatin</a>'s works, nearly verbatim. I can't believe how that guy</span>
  <span id="freeText9583478248823398972" style="display:none">Ha! I'm listening to the part where Ross is visiting Hank's farm (don't worry, that's not a spoiler)(and I'm cracking up that the reader gave Farmer Hank the voice of Hank Hill ("King of the Hill"))<br />EN EE way...so Hank is explaining how they're using crops and animals to restore the soil and how chickens run along behind to peck worms and grubs from the dirt and I am almost convinced that Suarez got this whole passage from one of <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/author/show/2623.Joel_Salatin" title="Joel Salatin" rel="nofollow noopener">Joel Salatin</a>'s works, nearly verbatim. I can't believe how that guy keeps coming back to haunt me. I mean, I can't argue that his methods don't seem right and sound but the dude just bugs the crap outta me and yet, he's always riiiiight theeeere...<br /><br />Alright, I'm done.<br /><br />I didn't like this as much as the first book and when it ended, I was all, "That was <i>it</i>?" <br />I dunno. It's still quite entertaining and offers plenty of food for thought, but...it's, in essence, a Disney fairy tale for growed-up boys. No, I don't mean Peter Pan, I mean violence and black-n-white and superheroes and Real Men and justice and good vs evil with badass machines that wield sharp knives.<br /><br /><a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[And the touchy-feely ending for CyborgLoki? I threw up all over the keyboard. Ok not really, but I felt like it. It was so squishy yet anticlimactic and it felt awfully rushed and wrong. Much like Ross-n-Natalie-sittin-in-a-tree. Yay that they found each other but did she even have a purpose in this story, other than to be rescued and to give Ivan Ross something more to do? Because, you know, women = barefoot and pregnant. Even the smart, capable ones. And Roy Merrit as Paul Bunyan as Superman (Yes, I KNOW he didn't play Paul Bunyan as Superman. I'm saying he was this super gigantic, heroic character made from the scraps of other players/inhabitants) upset me because he'd been my favorite in the first book regardless of his 2-D qualities but his representation in this book gave me a mindache.<br />Questions: Didn't NaziMan Berner (is that his name?) owe Loki so many favors? Like six of them or something? Did putting Loki back together count for all six and then Berner just became a rogue vigilante Dspace enforcer? What happened there aside from him now having the ability to beat people up? Then there's Seabeck (remember, I listened to this and also not looking at the character sheet that comes with the summary of this book so I could be spelling these names incorrectly) Does he really want to go back to his old life? I thought he was all tortured over that? And what happened with Angie Anderson? Do these unanswered questions mean there's another book coming, something that will tell us what became of these people and what the world looks like 50 years after this new societal construct? <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span><br /><br />I'm not quite as optimistic as the author. Yes, I have seen online sites regulate themselves nicely with popular opinion keeping trolls in line and such. Do I think that would work on a societal level and do I think that this new structure would work any better than previous regimes? No, I don't. There are flaws and the biggest problem with any human community is that it involves people. People are capable of doing amazing things and just as capable at being horrific and when you put them all together, you probably get some sort of weird balance, one that defies all attempts at imposing order and harmony because that just cannot exist absolutely when people are involved.<br />I worried more about what would happen when this technology became obsolete and how quickly that would happen. Yes, Sobel created this environment to allow for growth and intergenerational functionality but what happens when there are too few programmers who care about moving old data to a new system? What happens when everyone becomes too wrapped up in their own little things to care about the larger community, creating gaps that can be filled by anyone willing to manipulate the system? How will this system continue to thwart hackers or the next Sobel who is even smarter and more capable and who creates a rival system? How long will this flourishing new society survive? Even though I would love to live in a world with renewable resource management and viable community gardening and water treatment and conservation plans and think tanks of every-peoples working for the betterment of society, I am sad that a scenario like this probably wouldn't really work. And actually? This is way too much unloving pondering for a review. <br /><br />It's neat that I thought about all these things while I listened. Again, it was enjoyable and entertaining and I like this particular reader; he was a good choice for this book.</span>
  <a data-text-id="9583478248823398972" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/8802423-david-sven?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer376490983" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6137879686878811690">Wow. Reading this book as well as the previous book <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4699575.Daemon__Daemon___1_' title='Daemon (Daemon, #1) by Daniel Suarez' rel='nofollow noopener'>Daemon</a> (my review <a target='_blank' rel='noopener nofollow' href='https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/366358735'>http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/...</a>) has made me realize how much technology has been integrated into our modern lives as a virtual world overlaying our real world. We have internet banking, email, twitter, facebook, sms, mms, all manner of social networking, remote computing and giving us instant access to information all from a mobile phone or Ipad or laptop. We can multi-task our real lives and our virtual lives to mak</span>
  <span id="freeText6137879686878811690" style="display:none">Wow. Reading this book as well as the previous book <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/4699575.Daemon__Daemon___1_" title="Daemon (Daemon, #1) by Daniel Suarez" rel="nofollow noopener">Daemon</a> (my review <a target="_blank" rel="noopener nofollow" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/366358735">http://www.goodreads.com/review/show/...</a>) has made me realize how much technology has been integrated into our modern lives as a virtual world overlaying our real world. We have internet banking, email, twitter, facebook, sms, mms, all manner of social networking, remote computing and giving us instant access to information all from a mobile phone or Ipad or laptop. We can multi-task our real lives and our virtual lives to make one almost indistinguishable from the other.<br /><br />Now imagine all that technology built into your clothes and viewed through sun glasses or visors or contact lenses displaying information in a HUD interface just like an MMO but for real life. Welcome to D-Space, the Daemon's user interface for the "darknet." In here users interact with virtual apps, objects and programs which control real objects using a combination of voice commands and gestures to chant spells (passwords etc) or call down hell fire from high-tech weaponized drones in the stratosphere. <br /><br />The darknet, introduced to us in the last book is an interactive INTRANET available only to darknet members and modelled on Matthew Sobol's online MMO, complete with its own economy of darknet credits and levelling system, both of which are used as rewards for contributing to the spread of the Daemon's infrastructure and ideology. The higher the users credits or levels, the greater the access to the system enabling more technology and equipment to be created, ordered and used.<br /><br />The last book left us out of breath from the pace and action as the Daemon usurps entire corporations to take over the resources of greedy multi national companies and draft them into the expansion of the darknet. In this book its The Daemon vs The corporations. The faceless men who are the unelected puppet masters behind the illusion of democratic government. <br /><br />In the Red corner we have The Major, the front man for the corporations who heads up the private security firms that have been contracted by the government for . . . ? Well for security one would think.<br />In the Dark corner we have the return of sargeant Sebeck, recruited against his will by the Daemon to conduct Matthew Sobol's quest to see if humanity deserves to be free. Piece of cake. We also have in the dark corner, Loki Stormbringer, a 50+ level sorcerer and umpteenth level sociopath, with his army of unmanned killing machines, acting as the official enforcer and protector of the darknet infrastructure. <br />Also in the dark Corner we have Ron Merritt - yes he did die last book, yes he is really dead - but then so is Matthew Sobol. <br /><br />Und also in ze Dark Corner ve haf SS Officer Heinrich Boerner. A personal favourite uf mein. Yez, yu schweinhund, he ist an NPC in Herr Sobols computer game. But apparently he haz vays and meanz of making you talk, or scream or vat efer else before you <i>DIE!!!</i>. <br /><br />Continues straight on from the last book. The pace is still good. The action is still as visceral. But is the Daemon evil? You will have to read and decide for yourself <i>bwahahahaha</i><br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="6137879686878811690" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/100766825" />
    <a title="Adam McDonald" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3575511-adam-mcdonald"><img alt="Adam McDonald" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/m_50x66-82093808bca726cb3249a493fbd3bd0f.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/100766825?book_show_action=true">Apr 30, 2010</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Adam McDonald" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Adam McDonald" href="/user/show/3575511-adam-mcdonald">Adam McDonald</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer100766825" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer6912855822257746650">My review has to come in 2 parts, but before I get to it, I must make one thing clear. In order to understand and get the most out of this book, you MUST read its prequel, DAEMON, before you pick this one up. If you don't you will have no idea about the plot, characters, etc. So read DAEMON first!<br/><br/>Now, for the first part. I was originally only going to give this book 3 stars, not because it's not a good or enjoyable read, but because a lor of what takes place relies heavily on the reader's experi</span>
  <span id="freeText6912855822257746650" style="display:none">My review has to come in 2 parts, but before I get to it, I must make one thing clear. In order to understand and get the most out of this book, you MUST read its prequel, DAEMON, before you pick this one up. If you don't you will have no idea about the plot, characters, etc. So read DAEMON first!<br /><br />Now, for the first part. I was originally only going to give this book 3 stars, not because it's not a good or enjoyable read, but because a lor of what takes place relies heavily on the reader's experience with MMOG's (massive multiplayer online games) like World of Warcraft, Everquest, etc. I completely enjoyed it, but I thought others might not due to this fact.<br /><br />I finally decided on 5 stars, not because these themes became less important throughout the story, but because he lightened up on the gaming aspect and showed how his including them in the story did have a lot to do with the plot, and will eventually make perfect sense, even to the non-gamer crowd.<br /><br />I am deliberately avoiding mentioning what takes place because it would be nigh impossible to avoid spoilers, and this is a story that I would not want to ruin by revealing plot points, but let me just say it takes everything you learned from the first book, stands it on its head, and then shakes it into oblivion.<br /><br />I wish a few subplots had been more thoroughly fleshed out, and that the ending was less ambivalent, but if you enjoyed the first book, or are looking for interesting insights on society, civilization, the free market, socialism, etc then you should enjoy this book as much as I did.</span>
  <a data-text-id="6912855822257746650" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer10176055988914998484"><em>[Yeah, spoilers. Boilerplate, polite version: I promise I don't "spoil" anything about this book that would have bothered me had I known about it in advance of reading this book. That said, I cannot think of anything I have read in my life that would have been spoiled had I known the plot-advancing facts. And this is not, I promise, a brief Cliffs Notes–style detailed summary of the story. Perhaps the only real way to "spoil" a book is to detail any serious flaws in logic, to the extent that you</em></span>
  <span id="freeText10176055988914998484" style="display:none"><em>[Yeah, spoilers. Boilerplate, polite version: I promise I don't "spoil" anything about this book that would have bothered me had I known about it in advance of reading this book. That said, I cannot think of anything I have read in my life that would have been spoiled had I known the plot-advancing facts. And this is not, I promise, a brief Cliffs Notes–style detailed summary of the story. Perhaps the only real way to "spoil" a book is to detail any serious flaws in logic, to the extent that you then can't get them out of your head as you read the book. I can't promise that I don't to that -- but neither can anyone else.]</em><br /><br />This book is a sequel to Daemon. Unfortunately, it is less of a novel and more of a series of anecdotal extensions from its predecessor, dropped along a plot line depicting one of those characters making an extensive journey. If you haven't read Daemon, there's no sense in reading this. If you have, the primary benefit is how the playing field shifts, and how the Daemon of the original story is revealed to be a much more neutral presence than it previously seemed to be. Not neutral in terms of impact, neutral in terms of good/bad and right/wrong.</span>

      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Mass Market Paperback 9780451231895" href="/book/show/8488830-freedom">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83126923-adam?shelf=sci-fi">sci-fi</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/83126923-adam?shelf=non-mm">non-mm</a>

<span id="freeTextContainer860911559711706489">I have held off starting on the second part of this series because I didn't like how the story evolved by the end of the first book. I still hated the introduction of the razorbacks and the cyber-genetic implants as it undermined the realism of the story for me.<br/><br/>The first book focused a large portion on technology and being a little of a tech geek, I was enthralled by the accuracy of the technology imbued with the evolution of the Daemon. Combining my love of gaming which was heavily referenced, </span>
  <span id="freeText860911559711706489" style="display:none">I have held off starting on the second part of this series because I didn't like how the story evolved by the end of the first book. I still hated the introduction of the razorbacks and the cyber-genetic implants as it undermined the realism of the story for me.<br /><br />The first book focused a large portion on technology and being a little of a tech geek, I was enthralled by the accuracy of the technology imbued with the evolution of the Daemon. Combining my love of gaming which was heavily referenced, I was immediately sold to the concept of the book.<br />However the second book sees a shift from technology to instead focus on the impact of the Daemon on the world, the implications to economy, political systems and most of all, the people. I didn't really mind the shift because it was really well written, and wow, I did not expect that I would actually love the story more than the first book! <br /><br />There were some parts of the book where I was starting to get a little bored of the story. Then the book suddenly drops a bombshell on you to shock and hook you again. Loki's <a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[torture scene was a little overwhelming for me, thankfully it's a book and I don't have to visually picture the scene. <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span> <br /><br />This book started to showcase the positive side the Daemon could bring and I really loved Hank's story arc about his farm. It was heartwarming and it just resonated with me. <br /><br />The last few chapters of the books were really thrilling and I was extremely hooked. There was non-stop action filled with emotional scenes and it was all over before I even knew it. The whole series has been a hell of a ride and I was so glad I picked it up!</span>
  <a data-text-id="860911559711706489" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<span id="freeTextContainer14770904098451192437">A surprisingly better book than the original which has a more realistic theme and plot than it’s predecessor. Still lots of techie and video game feel with a more modern anti-corporation message.<br/>Definitely feels more relevant after having experienced the darker side of social media/ tech companies the past few years.</span>

  function submitShelfLink(unique_id, book_id, shelf_id, shelf_name, submit_form, exclusive) {
    var checkbox_id = 'shelf_name_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    var element = document.getElementById(checkbox_id)

    var checked = element.checked
    if (checked && exclusive) {
      // can't uncheck a radio by clicking it!
      return
    }
    if(document.getElementById("savingMessage")){
      Element.show('savingMessage')
    }
    var element_id = 'shelfInDropdownName_' + unique_id + '_' + shelf_id;
    Element.update(element_id, "saving...");
    if (submit_form) {
      Element.hide('shelfDropdown_' + unique_id)
      var form = document.getElementById('addBookForm' + book_id)
      if (form) {
        form.shelf.value = shelf_name
        form.onsubmit()
      }
    }
    else {
      var action = checked ? 'remove' : ''
      element.checked = !element.checked
      new Ajax.Request('/shelf/add_to_shelf', {asynchronous:true, evalScripts:true, onSuccess:function(request){shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name)}, parameters:'book_id=' + book_id + '&name=' + shelf_name + '&a=' + action + '&authenticity_token=' + encodeURIComponent('NdjiWJGkBM82BN1f8iiMYDzJiNGEyHf6wfD+eTUE+OhpcYoZq7YV76unW1UXtO4DmURp+tHYyq5UQqav7qM88A==')})
    }
  }

  function shelfSubmitted(request, book_id, checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name) {
    Element.update('shelfListfalse_' + book_id, request.responseText)
    afterShelfSave(checkbox_id, element_id, unique_id, shelf_name.escapeHTML())
  }

  function refreshGroupBox(group_id, book_id) {

  }

<div class='bookAuthorProfile__about'>

<span id="freeTextContainer10360036468528252056">DANIEL SUAREZ is the author of the New York Times bestseller Daemon, Freedom™, Kill Decision, and Influx. A former systems consultant to Fortune 1000 companies, he has designed and developed mission-critical software for the defense, finance, and entertainment industries. With a lifelong interest in both IT systems and creative writing, his high-tech and Sci-Fi thrillers focus on technology-driven</span>
  <span id="freeText10360036468528252056" style="display:none">DANIEL SUAREZ is the author of the New York Times bestseller Daemon, Freedom™, Kill Decision, and Influx. A former systems consultant to Fortune 1000 companies, he has designed and developed mission-critical software for the defense, finance, and entertainment industries. With a lifelong interest in both IT systems and creative writing, his high-tech and Sci-Fi thrillers focus on technology-driven change. Suarez is a past speaker at TED Global, MIT Media Lab, NASA Ames, the Long Now Foundation, and the headquarters of Google, Microsoft, and Amazon -- among many others. Self-taught in software development, he is a graduate from University of Delaware with a BA in English Literature. An avid PC and console gamer, his own world-building skills were bolstered through years as a pen &amp; paper role-playing game moderator. He lives in Los Angeles, California. </span>

Thousands of autonomous computer programs, or daemons, make our networked world possible, running constantly in the background of our lives, trafficking e-mail, transferring money, and monitoring power grids. For the most part, daemons are benign, but the same can\'t always be said for the people who design them. Matthew Sobol was a legendary computer game designer—the architect behind half-a-dozen popular online games. His premature death depressed both gamers and his company\'s stock price. But Sobol\'s fans aren\'t the only ones to note his passing. When his obituary is posted online, a previously dormant daemon activates, initiating a chain of events intended to unravel the fabric of our hyper-efficient, interconnected world. With Sobol\'s secrets buried along with him, and as new layers of his daemon are unleashed at every turn, it\'s up to an unlikely alliance to decipher his intricate plans and wrest the world from the grasp of a nameless, faceless enemy—or learn to live in a society in which we are no longer in control. . . . Computer technology expert Daniel Suarez blends haunting high-tech realism with gripping suspense in an authentic, complex thriller in the tradition of Michael Crichton, Neal Stephenson, and William
